<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Staircase Detail Engineering</h1>
  <p>
A staircase is not a circulation symbol. It is a structural and dimensional system that must resolve vertical movement with safety, comfort, and load transfer logic. In internship portfolios, stair details reveal whether the student understands riser-tread proportion, landing support, slab integration, and structural anchoring.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-zone">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1505693416388-ac5ce068fe85?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="strip">
  <div class="content">
    <h3>Purpose of Stair Detail</h3>
    <p>
The stair detail must demonstrate riser height consistency, tread width proportion, landing dimension clarity, slab junction, and structural continuity. It must not appear as a symbolic zig-zag without structural depth.
    </p>

    <h3>Dimensional Logic</h3>
    <p>
Indicate riser height, tread width, total rise, landing width, and stair width. Avoid excessive internal dimensioning. Maintain clarity and hierarchy.
    </p>

    <h3>Structural Representation</h3>
    <p>
Show slab thickness supporting stair. Indicate beam connection if applicable. Represent load path visually. Avoid floating stairs without support logic.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="side">
    Staircase proportion must follow ergonomic logic.  
    Landing alignment must correspond to grid discipline.  
    Section must reveal connection between slab and stair body.  
    Annotation must remain secondary to structure.
  </div>
</section>

<section class="strip alt">
  <div class="content">
    <h3>Common Errors</h3>
    <p>
Uniform thin lines, missing structural anchoring, inconsistent riser height, unrealistic cantilever stair projections, and absence of slab thickness reduce technical credibility.
    </p>

    <h3>Scale Discipline</h3>
    <p>
Stair details in internship portfolios should appear at 1:20 or 1:10. Avoid presenting staircase at 1:100 if demonstrating technical precision.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=stair%20detail%20architecture">
      ArchDaily Stair Detail Benchmark →
    </a>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20stair%20detail%20portfolio">
      Student Stair Detail Portfolio Examples →
    </a>
  </div>

  <div class="side">
    Stair detail must reflect:  
    • Riser-tread ratio discipline  
    • Structural anchoring  
    • Section clarity  
    • Dimension precision  
    • Load transfer awareness
  </div>
</section>

<section class="strip">
  <div class="content">
    <div class="accordion">
      <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Staircase Detail Checklist</button>
      <div>
        <p>
Is riser height consistent?  
Is tread width dimensioned?  
Is landing clearly supported?  
Is slab thickness visible?  
Are annotations lighter than structure?  
Is scale indicated?
        </p>
      </div>
    </div>
  </div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Toilet Detail Standards</h1>
  <p>
Toilet detailing is one of the most revealing technical indicators in an internship portfolio. It demonstrates coordination between architecture, structure, waterproofing, plumbing, and finishes. Weak toilet details indicate surface-level drafting. Strong toilet details signal real-world constructability awareness.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-band">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1499951360447-b19be8fe80f5?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band">
  <div class="content">
    <h3>Purpose of Toilet Detail</h3>
    <p>
A toilet detail must communicate slab depression logic, waterproofing layers, floor finish thickness, plumbing routing, and threshold transition. It must not be a symbolic plan without construction layering.
    </p>

    <h3>Floor Depression Logic</h3>
    <p>
Indicate lowered slab level relative to adjacent room. Show waterproof membrane. Demonstrate slope towards floor trap. Clearly represent tile thickness and mortar bed.
    </p>

    <h3>Wall Junction Clarity</h3>
    <p>
Show wall tiling height, waterproofing overlap, and junction between slab and wall. Avoid thin wall representations that ignore material layering.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="aside">
    Toilet detail must reflect:  
    • Waterproofing logic  
    • Plumbing integration  
    • Finish layering  
    • Threshold control  
    • Realistic slab thickness  
  </div>
</section>

<section class="band alt">
  <div class="content">
    <h3>Dimensioning Discipline</h3>
    <p>
Include floor-to-ceiling height, slab thickness, tile thickness, and key fixture distances. Avoid clutter. Dimension must remain thinner than structural cut.
    </p>

    <h3>Common Errors</h3>
    <p>
Missing waterproof layer, no slope indication, unrealistic wall thickness, floating fixtures without support logic, and over-annotation reduce credibility.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=toilet%20detail%20architecture">
      ArchDaily Toilet Detail Benchmark →
    </a>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20toilet%20detail%20portfolio">
      Student Toilet Detail Portfolio Examples →
    </a>
  </div>

  <div class="aside">
    Good toilet detail indicators:  
    • Clear slab depression  
    • Slope direction visible  
    • Waterproof membrane layered  
    • Fixtures dimensioned  
    • Structural logic maintained  
  </div>
</section>

<section class="band">
  <div class="content">
    <div class="accordion">
      <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Toilet Detail Checklist</button>
      <div>
        <p>
Is slab depression visible?  
Is waterproofing layer indicated?  
Is slope towards drain shown?  
Are wall tiles and finish layers clear?  
Are dimensions readable?  
Is structure visually dominant?
        </p>
      </div>
    </div>
  </div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Annotation Systems</h1>
  <p>
Annotations convert drawings from visual representation into communicative construction documents. Clear annotation systems demonstrate drafting maturity and coordination awareness. Weak annotation overwhelms structure or competes with hierarchy.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-section">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="split">
  <div>
    <h3>Purpose of Annotation</h3>
    <p>
Annotations must clarify material, dimension, level, and construction logic without overpowering structural hierarchy. They must be legible, restrained, and consistently formatted.
    </p>

    <h3>Annotation Hierarchy</h3>
    <p>
Primary notes — bold and minimal.  
Secondary notes — lighter weight.  
Material tags — aligned and consistent.  
Leaders — thin and controlled.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div>
    <h3>Placement Rules</h3>
    <p>
Place annotations outside drawing boundary when possible. Maintain consistent offset from structure. Avoid crossing leader lines. Keep text horizontal and readable.
    </p>

    <h3>Clarity at Print Scale</h3>
    <p>
Annotations must remain readable at final print size. Test at 100% print scale. If text requires zoom to read, reduce drawing density or increase annotation clarity.
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="split alt">
  <div>
    <h3>Common Errors</h3>
    <p>
Excessive text blocks.  
Overlapping leader lines.  
Annotation heavier than structural lines.  
Inconsistent font size.  
Mixed terminology.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architectural%20working%20drawing">
      ArchDaily Working Drawing Benchmark →
    </a>
  </div>

  <div>
    <h3>Software Discipline</h3>
    <p>
AutoCAD — maintain dimension style consistency and text height control.  
Revit — adjust annotation scale and view templates.  
Illustrator — maintain vector clarity and consistent stroke weight.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=technical%20architecture%20portfolio">
      Student Technical Portfolio Examples →
    </a>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
  <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Annotation System Checklist</button>
  <div>
    <p>
Is annotation thinner than structure?  
Is font hierarchy consistent?  
Are leader lines controlled?  
Is terminology precise?  
Is text readable at print scale?  
Does annotation support structure instead of competing with it?
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

  <a href="../contact.html">Contact</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Drawing Legibility at Print Scale</h1>
  <p>
Drawings are evaluated at print scale, not screen zoom. A technically correct drawing can fail if legibility collapses when printed. Internship reviewers frequently assess portfolios on physical printouts or reduced PDF thumbnails. Clarity must survive reduction.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-wrap">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1519681393784-d120267933ba?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="block">
  <div>
    <h3>Why Legibility Matters</h3>
    <p>
Legibility determines whether structural hierarchy, dimensions, and annotation remain clear when scaled down. If walls merge with projection lines or text becomes unreadable, technical credibility weakens.
    </p>

    <h3>Critical Tests</h3>
    <p>
Print one page at actual size.  
Zoom PDF to 25%.  
View on laptop screen at normal distance.  
Scroll rapidly and observe clarity.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div>
    <h3>Common Legibility Failures</h3>
    <p>
Text smaller than 6–7 pt equivalent.  
Dimension lines too thin to read.  
Excessive hatch density.  
Overlapping linework.  
Overcrowded layout.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20section%20detail">
      ArchDaily Section Legibility Benchmark →
    </a>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="block alt">
  <div>
    <h3>Line Weight and Scale Balance</h3>
    <p>
Cut lines must remain dominant even at reduced size. Projection lines should not visually merge. Hatch patterns must not overpower structural clarity.
    </p>

    <h3>Annotation Readability</h3>
    <p>
Annotation must remain readable without zoom. Maintain consistent text size across drawings. Avoid scaling text disproportionately during layout export.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div>
    <h3>Page Density Control</h3>
    <p>
Reduce number of drawings per page if clarity suffers. Increase white space. Enlarge strongest drawing rather than compressing multiple small drawings.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=technical%20architecture%20portfolio">
      Technical Portfolio Layout Comparison →
    </a>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
  <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Print Scale Checklist</button>
  <div>
    <p>
Are all dimensions readable?  
Is structural hierarchy visible?  
Does the drawing remain clear at 25% zoom?  
Is text crisp and vector-based?  
Are hatch patterns controlled?  
Is there sufficient white space?
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

  <a href="v06-ch77.html">Next — Chapter 77</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Avoiding Common Technical Errors</h1>
  <p>
Technical errors in internship portfolios are rarely conceptual. They are procedural. Reviewers identify recurring drafting mistakes that indicate insufficient professional discipline. Eliminating these errors significantly improves portfolio credibility.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-block">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1526401485004-2fda9f0d8c2d?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="axis">
  <div>
    <h3>Frequent Plan Errors</h3>
    <p>
Missing structural grid.  
No overall dimension.  
Uniform line weight.  
No north arrow.  
Inconsistent wall thickness.
    </p>

    <h3>Frequent Section Errors</h3>
    <p>
No visible slab thickness.  
Beam not shown.  
Floating cantilever.  
Overloaded hatch.  
No height dimension.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="axis-line"></div>

  <div>
    <h3>Detail Drawing Errors</h3>
    <p>
Missing material layering.  
Excessive annotation.  
No scale reference.  
Overly decorative shading.
    </p>

    <h3>Annotation Errors</h3>
    <p>
Text too small.  
Inconsistent font size.  
Dimension overlapping lines.  
Mixed units (mm and meters).
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20detail%20drawing">
      Professional Detail Drawing Benchmark →
    </a>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="panel-section">
  <div>
    <h3>Export Errors</h3>
    <p>
Rasterized text.  
Blurry PDF export.  
Incorrect page scaling.  
Compressed line weight.
    </p>

    <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=technical%20architecture%20portfolio">
      Technical Portfolio Comparison →
    </a>
  </div>

  <div>
    <h3>Layout Errors</h3>
    <p>
Overcrowded page.  
No focal hierarchy.  
Inconsistent margins.  
Misaligned drawings.
    </p>

    <h3>Hierarchy Errors</h3>
    <p>
Render dominating technical clarity.  
No sectional emphasis.  
Equal visual weight everywhere.
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
  <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Technical Error Audit Checklist</button>
  <div>
    <p>
Is structural grid present?  
Are dimensions readable?  
Is slab thickness visible?  
Is scale clearly indicated?  
Is export vector-based?  
Does drawing remain legible at 25% zoom?
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Balancing Render and Drafting Exposure</h1>
  <p>
Internship portfolios must balance visual presentation with measurable technical clarity. Overemphasis on renders signals surface-level engagement. Overemphasis on drafting without spatial visualization signals incomplete communication. Balance demonstrates maturity.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-wrap">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band">
  <div class="two-col">
    <div>
      <h3>Render Overexposure Risks</h3>
      <p>
Render-heavy portfolios often lack measurable information.  
No dimensions.  
No sections.  
No structural clarity.  
This suggests visual bias over buildable understanding.
      </p>
    </div>

    <div>
      <h3>Drafting Overexposure Risks</h3>
      <p>
Purely technical sheets without spatial visualization may feel rigid.  
No experiential depth.  
No atmospheric understanding.  
No spatial storytelling.
      </p>
    </div>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="band alt">
  <div class="two-col">
    <div class="card">
      <h3>Balanced Portfolio Indicators</h3>
      <p>
● Section within first three pages  
● One controlled render per project  
● Dimensioned plans  
● At least one detail  
● Spatial clarity supported by structure
      </p>
      <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20internship%20portfolio">
        Internship Portfolio Benchmark →
      </a>
    </div>

    <div class="card">
      <h3>Professional Benchmark Study</h3>
      <p>
Study ArchDaily technical clarity versus render dominance.  
Compare institutional projects and competition boards.  
Observe balance between image and documentation.
      </p>
      <a class="ref-card" target="_blank" href="https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20section%20drawing">
        Section Benchmark Reference →
      </a>
    </div>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
  <button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Balance Audit Checklist</button>
  <div>
    <p>
Does each project include at least one strong section?  
Are renders limited and purposeful?  
Are plans dimensioned?  
Is technical depth visible?  
Does the portfolio communicate both atmosphere and constructability?
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Grid Systems in Portfolio Design</h1>
  <p>
A portfolio without a grid is a collection.  
A portfolio with a grid is a system.  

Grid systems organize space the way structural grids organize buildings. They control alignment, hierarchy, spacing, rhythm, and visual coherence. In internship portfolios, inconsistent alignment signals indiscipline. A controlled grid signals architectural thinking applied to graphic communication.  

A grid is not visible decoration.  
It is invisible order.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="image-block">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1517816428104-797678c7cf7e?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="center-stack">

<div class="side-panel left-panel">
<strong>Why Grid Systems Matter</strong><br><br>
A grid ensures:<br>
● Consistent margins<br>
● Predictable alignment<br>
● Balanced white space<br>
● Typographic hierarchy<br>
● Drawing placement discipline<br><br>
Without a grid:<br>
● Titles shift unpredictably<br>
● Images float<br>
● Margins vary<br>
● Pages feel disconnected<br><br>
Reviewers may not consciously identify grid logic, but they immediately detect inconsistency.
</div>

<div class="content">

<div class="section">
<h3>Types of Grid Systems for Portfolios</h3>

<strong>Single Column Grid</strong><br>
Best for:<br>
● Minimal portfolios<br>
● Corporate-oriented archetypes<br>
● Strong section-based layouts<br>
Structure:<br>
● Wide margin<br>
● One central content column<br><br>

Benchmark reference:<br>
Behance search:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio%20layout<br>
Observe strict alignment and restrained composition.
</div>

<div class="section">
<strong>Two Column Grid</strong><br>
Best for:<br>
● Balanced portfolios<br>
● Text + drawing integration<br>
● Boutique archetypes<br>
Structure:<br>
● One column for drawing<br>
● One column for text or secondary diagrams<br><br>

Reference:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20grid%20layout<br>
Study how drawings and text align vertically.
</div>

<div class="section">
<strong>Modular Grid</strong><br>
Best for:<br>
● Urban portfolios<br>
● Diagram-heavy layouts<br>
● Data visualization<br>
Structure:<br>
● Multiple rows and columns<br>
● Equal modules<br>
● Controlled flexibility<br><br>

Reference:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20urban%20portfolio%20layout<br>
Observe mapping and diagram alignment.
</div>

</div>

<div class="side-panel right-panel">
<strong>Grid Construction Steps</strong><br><br>
1. Define page size (A4 or A3).<br>
2. Set margins.<br>
3. Define column count (1, 2, or modular).<br>
4. Establish baseline grid.<br>
5. Place recurring elements (title, page number).<br>
6. Lock template.<br><br>

Never design page-by-page without grid foundation.
</div>

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Checklist</button>
<div>
Are margins identical across pages?<br>
Do titles align consistently?<br>
Do drawings snap to grid?<br>
Is white space controlled?<br>
Does portfolio feel cohesive when scrolling?<br><br>
If grid inconsistencies exist, restructure before proceeding.<br><br>
Grid systems are structural frameworks for visual communication.<br>
They translate architectural order into graphic discipline.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Margin Control and White Space</h1>
<p>
White space is not emptiness.  
It is controlled breathing space that defines hierarchy, clarity, and confidence.  

In architectural portfolios, weak margin control creates visual noise. Strong white space management signals restraint, maturity, and structural thinking.  

White space is as important as drawings.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band">
<div>
<h3>Why Margin Control Matters</h3>
Margins create:<br>
● Visual stability<br>
● Page balance<br>
● Reading rhythm<br>
● Hierarchy clarity<br>
● Professional tone<br><br>

Inconsistent margins create:<br>
● Crowding<br>
● Floating content<br>
● Amateur layout impression<br><br>

Reviewers subconsciously evaluate margin discipline within seconds.
</div>
<div>
<h3>Standard Margin Framework</h3>
For A4 Portrait or Landscape:<br>
● Top margin: consistent across pages<br>
● Bottom margin: equal or slightly larger<br>
● Inner margin: controlled<br>
● Outer margin: slightly larger for breathing space<br><br>

For A3:<br>
● Margins must scale proportionally<br>
● Avoid shrinking margins to fit more content<br><br>

Benchmark minimal margin use:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20minimal%20layout<br>
Observe generous white space in high-quality portfolios.
</div>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div>
<h3>White Space as Hierarchy Tool</h3>
White space helps emphasize:<br>
● Primary drawing<br>
● Title<br>
● Section cut<br>
● Key diagram<br><br>

If everything is filled, nothing stands out.<br><br>

Study hierarchy through white space:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20white%20space<br>
Zoom out and observe visual breathing.
</div>
<div>
<h3>Avoiding Overcrowding</h3>
Common student mistake:<br>
● Multiple small drawings on one page<br>
● Text wrapped tightly around images<br>
● Margins reduced to fit content<br><br>

Instead:<br>
● Reduce number of drawings<br>
● Increase image size<br>
● Let page breathe<br><br>

Compare cluttered vs minimal portfolios:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20internship%20portfolio
</div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Checklist</button>
<div>
Are margins consistent across pages?<br>
Is white space evenly distributed?<br>
Does any drawing touch page edge?<br>
Is page density controlled?<br>
Does layout feel calm?<br><br>
If any answer is negative, adjust before moving forward.<br><br>
White space is structural silence.<br>
It strengthens clarity without adding content.
</div>
</section>

<aside class="global-nav">
<h3>Navigation</h3>
<a href="../index.html">Home</a>
<a href="../index-book.html">Master Index</a>

<a href="../author.html">Author</a>
<a href="../contact.html">Contact</a>
</aside>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Typography Hierarchy</h1>
<p>
Typography is structural communication.  
It determines how information is read, understood, and prioritized.  

In architectural portfolios, typography must remain subordinate to drawings while still providing clear navigation. Weak typography disrupts visual order. Strong typography reinforces hierarchy and coherence.  

Typography must be disciplined, restrained, and consistent.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-wrapper">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1524995997946-a1c2e315a42f?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="content">

<h3>Why Typography Hierarchy Matters</h3>
Typography controls:
<ul>
<li>Project titles</li>
<li>Section headings</li>
<li>Drawing labels</li>
<li>Page numbers</li>
<li>Captions</li>
<li>Annotation text</li>
</ul>

Without hierarchy:
<ul>
<li>Titles compete with drawings</li>
<li>Text appears random</li>
<li>Pages feel disorganized</li>
</ul>

Reviewers may not consciously analyze typography, but inconsistency immediately reduces perceived professionalism.

<h3>Basic Typography Hierarchy Structure</h3>
Three-level system recommended:

<strong>Primary Level</strong><br>
Project Title<br><br>

<strong>Secondary Level</strong><br>
Drawing Titles / Section Headers<br><br>

<strong>Tertiary Level</strong><br>
Body Text / Captions / Annotations<br><br>

Avoid more than three clear levels.

<h3>Project Title Standards</h3>
Project titles should:
<ul>
<li>Be largest text on page</li>
<li>Use consistent placement</li>
<li>Use bold or semi-bold weight</li>
<li>Avoid decorative fonts</li>
</ul>

Benchmark minimal portfolio typography:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20typography

<h3>Drawing Label Standards</h3>
Drawing labels must:
<ul>
<li>Be smaller than project title</li>
<li>Use consistent casing</li>
<li>Align consistently across pages</li>
<li>Include scale notation clearly</li>
</ul>

Reference:<br>
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20technical%20portfolio

<h3>Body Text Discipline</h3>
Design intent paragraphs should:
<ul>
<li>Be concise</li>
<li>Use smaller readable size</li>
<li>Maintain line spacing discipline</li>
<li>Align left</li>
</ul>

Avoid:
<ul>
<li>Full-width long paragraphs</li>
<li>Excessive line spacing</li>
<li>Center-aligned body text</li>
</ul>

Benchmark professional brevity:<br>
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Checklist</button>
<div>
Is there a clear three-level hierarchy?<br>
Is only one or two font families used?<br>
Are titles consistent across projects?<br>
Is text legible at print scale?<br>
Does typography support drawings rather than compete?<br><br>
Typography hierarchy is architectural hierarchy translated into text.<br>
It organizes perception with precision and restraint.
</div>
</section>

<aside class="global-nav">
<h3>Navigation</h3>
<a href="../index.html">Home</a>
<a href="../index-book.html">Master Index</a>
<a href="../about.html">About the Book</a>
<a href="../author.html">Author</a>
<a href="../contact.html">Contact</a>
</aside>

<div class="main">

<section class="header-strip">
<h1>Font Selection Strategy</h1>
<p>
Font selection defines tone.  
It silently communicates whether the portfolio is disciplined, experimental, corporate, or conceptual.  

In architectural portfolios, fonts must never dominate content. They must support drawings, structure information, and maintain consistency across the entire document.  

The goal is neutrality with precision.
</p>
</section>

<section class="visual-panel">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
<div>
<p>
Why Font Selection Matters  

Fonts influence:  
● Professional perception  
● Readability at print scale  
● Digital clarity  
● Visual hierarchy  
● Portfolio identity  

Weak font selection leads to:  
● Decorative appearance  
● Amateur impression  
● Inconsistent tone  
● Reduced credibility  

Strong font selection is often unnoticed — and that is its success.
</p>
</div>
</section>

<section class="content">

<div>

<h3>Core Font Selection Principles</h3>
Use:
<ul>
<li>One primary font family</li>
<li>Optional secondary weight within same family</li>
<li>Maximum two font families</li>
</ul>

Avoid:
<ul>
<li>Script fonts</li>
<li>Decorative display fonts</li>
<li>Handwritten-style fonts</li>
<li>More than two type families</li>
</ul>

Consistency outweighs uniqueness.

<h3>Recommended Font Categories</h3>

<strong>Neutral Sans-Serif</strong><br>
Best for:
<ul>
<li>Corporate-oriented portfolios</li>
<li>Technical-heavy portfolios</li>
<li>BIM-focused applications</li>
</ul>

Reference:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio

</div>

<div>

<strong>Humanist Sans-Serif</strong><br>
Best for:
<ul>
<li>Boutique studios</li>
<li>Sustainable portfolios</li>
<li>Design-led practices</li>
</ul>

Reference:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20student%20portfolio

<br><br>

<strong>Serif (Controlled Use Only)</strong><br>
Can be used for:
<ul>
<li>Title emphasis</li>
<li>Boutique portfolios</li>
</ul>

Avoid using serif for body text in technical portfolios.

Reference:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20typography

</div>

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Checklist</button>
<div>
Is only one primary font used?<br>
Are weights consistent?<br>
Is hierarchy clearly visible?<br>
Is text legible at print scale?<br>
Does font align with portfolio archetype?<br><br>
Font selection defines tone without drawing attention to itself.<br>
Restraint and consistency are the markers of professional maturity.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Color Discipline</h1>
<p>
Color is a powerful visual tool.  
In architectural portfolios, uncontrolled color creates distraction. Controlled color establishes hierarchy, clarity, and professional tone.  

Color must support drawings, not dominate them.  
Restraint in color communicates confidence.
</p>
</section>

<section class="spine-section">
<div class="split">

<div>

<h3>Why Color Discipline Matters</h3>
Color influences:
<ul>
<li>Visual hierarchy</li>
<li>Page rhythm</li>
<li>Diagram clarity</li>
<li>Professional tone</li>
<li>Brand consistency</li>
</ul>

Uncontrolled color results in:
<ul>
<li>Graphic noise</li>
<li>Overwhelmed drawings</li>
<li>Inconsistent identity</li>
<li>Amateur presentation</li>
</ul>

Professional portfolios use color strategically — not emotionally.

<h3>Primary Color Strategy</h3>
Recommended approach:
<ul>
<li>Black and white base</li>
<li>One accent color maximum</li>
<li>Use accent only for highlights or diagrams</li>
</ul>

Avoid:
<ul>
<li>Multiple bright colors</li>
<li>Gradient backgrounds</li>
<li>Heavy colored page fills</li>
</ul>

</div>

<div>

<h3>Color and Archetype Alignment</h3>

<strong>Corporate-Oriented</strong>
<ul>
<li>Neutral palette</li>
<li>Minimal accent use</li>
<li>High contrast drawings</li>
</ul>

<strong>Boutique</strong>
<ul>
<li>Subtle accent allowed</li>
<li>Controlled tone variation</li>
</ul>

<strong>Sustainable</strong>
<ul>
<li>Environmental diagrams may use restrained accent</li>
<li>Consistent legend color</li>
</ul>

<strong>Urban</strong>
<ul>
<li>Color-coded mapping systems</li>
<li>Legend clarity critical</li>
</ul>

<strong>Interior</strong>
<ul>
<li>Material board controlled palette</li>
<li>No oversaturated renders</li>
</ul>

Benchmark references:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20diagram%20portfolio  

</div>

</div>
</section>

<section class="image-band">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1507525428034-b723cf961d3e?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Checklist</button>
<div>
Is black and white dominant?<br>
Is accent color used sparingly?<br>
Are diagrams consistent in color logic?<br>
Does color reinforce hierarchy?<br>
Is palette aligned with portfolio archetype?<br><br>
Color discipline strengthens clarity through restraint.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Monochrome vs Accent Strategy</h1>
<p>
Color usage in architectural portfolios must be intentional.  
The decision between strict monochrome and controlled accent determines visual tone, hierarchy clarity, and professional perception.  

This chapter defines when to remain monochrome and when to introduce accent strategically.
</p>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div class="grid">

<div>
<h3>Monochrome Strategy</h3>

Monochrome portfolios rely on:
<ul>
<li>Black, white, and grey tones</li>
<li>Line weight hierarchy</li>
<li>White space control</li>
<li>Typography precision</li>
</ul>

Advantages:
<ul>
<li>Professional tone</li>
<li>High clarity</li>
<li>Focus on drawings</li>
<li>Timeless aesthetic</li>
</ul>

Best suited for:
<ul>
<li>Corporate-oriented firms</li>
<li>Technical portfolios</li>
<li>BIM-heavy applications</li>
</ul>

Reference:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=monochrome%20architecture%20portfolio
</div>

<div>
<h3>Accent Strategy</h3>

Accent color must:
<ul>
<li>Highlight diagrams</li>
<li>Emphasize key zones</li>
<li>Support navigation</li>
<li>Remain limited to one tone</li>
</ul>

Rules:
<ul>
<li>Do not exceed one accent color</li>
<li>Do not fill backgrounds heavily</li>
<li>Keep drawings dominant</li>
<li>Maintain consistency across pages</li>
</ul>

Best suited for:
<ul>
<li>Boutique studios</li>
<li>Sustainable portfolios</li>
<li>Urban mapping-heavy layouts</li>
</ul>

Reference:
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20diagram%20portfolio
</div>

</div>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1523731407965-2430cd12f5e4?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band alt">
<div class="grid">

<div>
<h3>When to Stay Monochrome</h3>
<ul>
<li>If drawings rely heavily on line precision</li>
<li>If applying to execution-focused firm</li>
<li>If typography and grid are primary strengths</li>
<li>If unsure about color calibration</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div>
<h3>When to Introduce Accent</h3>
<ul>
<li>If diagrams require zone distinction</li>
<li>If environmental overlays need clarity</li>
<li>If navigation requires subtle highlight</li>
<li>If visual identity requires reinforcement</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Calibration Checklist</button>
<div>
Is monochrome dominant?<br>
Is accent limited to one tone?<br>
Does accent support hierarchy rather than dominate?<br>
Is color consistent across all projects?<br>
Does palette align with intended firm type?<br><br>
Strategic restraint defines professional color maturity.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Image Placement Systems</h1>
<p>
Images dominate architectural portfolios.  
Placement determines hierarchy, clarity, and narrative pacing.  
A strong placement system ensures drawings lead the eye logically without visual conflict.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-panel">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1503387762-592deb58ef4e?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
<div class="text">
<h3>Primary Image Rule</h3>
<ul>
<li>One dominant image per page</li>
<li>Secondary elements support, not compete</li>
<li>Maintain consistent margins</li>
<li>Avoid edge-touching visuals</li>
<li>Align to grid system</li>
</ul>
</div>
</section>

<section class="section">
<h3>Common Image Placement Models</h3>

<ul>
<li><strong>Full-Width Anchor:</strong> Ideal for overview pages. Use for strongest visual only.</li>
<li><strong>Two-Column Balance:</strong> One drawing, one supporting diagram or text.</li>
<li><strong>Vertical Stack:</strong> Concept → Plan → Section progression.</li>
<li><strong>Modular Grid:</strong> Used for urban mapping or multi-diagram layouts.</li>
<li><strong>Offset Composition:</strong> Controlled asymmetry anchored to grid.</li>
</ul>

Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20layout

</section>

<section class="section" style="background:#fafafa;">
<h3>Image Hierarchy Discipline</h3>

<ul>
<li>Primary drawing largest on page</li>
<li>Secondary diagram 60–70% of primary scale</li>
<li>Tertiary captions minimal</li>
<li>No equal-size visual competition</li>
<li>White space frames visual dominance</li>
</ul>

Study presentation boards:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20presentation%20board

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Calibration Checklist</button>
<div>
Does each page have one dominant image?<br>
Are images aligned consistently across pages?<br>
Is white space preserved around drawings?<br>
Is image scale appropriate to page size?<br>
Does placement reinforce project hierarchy?<br><br>
Image placement defines reading sequence.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Modular Layout Logic</h1>
<p>
A modular layout system divides the page into measurable units.  
Each drawing, diagram, and text block occupies defined modules.  
Consistency of modules produces visual rhythm and structural discipline.
</p>
</section>

<section class="matrix">

<div>
<h3>Module Discipline</h3>
<ul>
<li>Equal base units</li>
<li>Consistent gutters</li>
<li>Predictable spacing</li>
<li>Controlled variation</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div>
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1497366216548-37526070297c?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div>
<h3>Grid Ratios</h3>
<ul>
<li>1:1 modules</li>
<li>2x vertical spans</li>
<li>3x anchor panels</li>
<li>Balanced asymmetry</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div>
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1505691723518-36a5ac3b2d95?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

</section>

<section class="section">
<h3>When to Use Modular Systems</h3>

<ul>
<li>Urban mapping pages</li>
<li>Multi-diagram presentations</li>
<li>Technical documentation spreads</li>
<li>Comparative analysis layouts</li>
<li>Data-heavy environmental studies</li>
</ul>

Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20grid%20layout

</section>

<section class="section" style="background:#fafafa;">
<h3>Common Errors</h3>

<ul>
<li>Breaking module alignment mid-page</li>
<li>Irregular gutter spacing</li>
<li>Overloading too many modules</li>
<li>No dominant module hierarchy</li>
<li>Ignoring margin consistency</li>
</ul>

Study urban diagram structure:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=urban%20analysis%20diagram

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Modular Calibration Checklist</button>
<div>
Are all elements aligned to base grid?<br>
Are gutters consistent?<br>
Is there one dominant module per page?<br>
Is white space preserved between modules?<br>
Does layout remain legible at 25% zoom?<br><br>
Modular logic converts graphic freedom into measurable order.
</div>
</section>

<a href="v07-ch87.html">Next — Chapter 87</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Page Rhythm Engineering</h1>
<p>
Page rhythm defines how visual density alternates across a portfolio.  
A well-engineered rhythm prevents monotony and maintains reader engagement.  
Rhythm is achieved through controlled variation in scale, spacing, and composition.
</p>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div class="band-text">
<h3>Density Alternation</h3>
<ul>
<li>Alternate heavy and light pages</li>
<li>Balance text and drawing weight</li>
<li>Control image dominance</li>
<li>Avoid repetitive layouts</li>
<li>Introduce spatial pauses</li>
</ul>

Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20layout
</div>
<div class="band-image">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div class="band-image">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1529429611278-4a8e6d1e4c44?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
<div class="band-text">
<h3>Sequential Variation</h3>
<ul>
<li>Overview page → Plan page</li>
<li>Plan page → Section page</li>
<li>Section page → Diagram page</li>
<li>Diagram page → Detail page</li>
<li>Detail page → Render page</li>
</ul>

Study scroll rhythm via:  
https://issuu.com/search?q=architecture%20portfolio
</div>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div class="band-text">
<h3>Rhythm Control Indicators</h3>
<ul>
<li>No three identical layouts consecutively</li>
<li>Section appears early</li>
<li>White space acts as pause</li>
<li>Technical sheets punctuate narrative</li>
<li>Closing page remains disciplined</li>
</ul>

ArchDaily reference:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20presentation%20board
</div>
<div class="band-image">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1503387762-592deb58ef4e?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Rhythm Calibration Checklist</button>
<div>
Does each page differ structurally from previous?<br>
Is density alternated intentionally?<br>
Are heavy diagram pages followed by breathing space?<br>
Does scrolling feel balanced rather than repetitive?<br>
Is strongest project rhythmically positioned early?<br><br>
Rhythm transforms static documentation into visual sequence.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Alignment Discipline</h1>
<p>
Alignment is structural order translated into graphic space.  
Misalignment introduces visual instability.  
Precise alignment reinforces hierarchy, rhythm, and credibility.
</p>
</section>

<section class="block">
<div>
<h3>Horizontal Alignment Logic</h3>
<ul>
<li>Titles align to consistent margin</li>
<li>Image edges snap to grid lines</li>
<li>Caption baselines remain constant</li>
<li>Page numbers follow fixed axis</li>
<li>Repeated elements occupy identical coordinates</li>
</ul>

Reference Study:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20grid%20layout
</div>
<div>
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1481277542470-605612bd2d61?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
</section>

<section class="block">
<div>
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1501594907352-04cda38ebc29?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
<div>
<h3>Vertical Axis Discipline</h3>
<ul>
<li>Text blocks share left boundary</li>
<li>Images align across spreads</li>
<li>Columns follow invisible structural grid</li>
<li>Baseline grid maintains text rhythm</li>
<li>No floating elements</li>
</ul>

Issuu Alignment Study:  
https://issuu.com/search?q=architecture%20portfolio
</div>
</section>

<section class="block">
<div>
<h3>Alignment Failure Indicators</h3>
<ul>
<li>Titles shift between pages</li>
<li>Uneven image margins</li>
<li>Random caption placement</li>
<li>Inconsistent spacing</li>
<li>Irregular grid snapping</li>
</ul>

ArchDaily Board Study:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20presentation%20board
</div>
<div>
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1523419409543-a5e549c1c89b?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Alignment Audit Checklist</button>
<div>
Do titles align consistently across pages?<br>
Do image edges snap to the same margin?<br>
Is baseline grid consistent for text?<br>
Are captions positioned uniformly?<br>
Is any element visually drifting?<br><br>
Alignment discipline reinforces professional perception instantly.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Avoiding Graphic Noise</h1>
<p>
Graphic noise is visual clutter that weakens clarity, hierarchy, and professionalism.  
In architectural portfolios, excessive effects, inconsistent styling, and uncontrolled density reduce 
credibility. Refinement is achieved through subtraction, not decoration.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1600&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="text-stack">

<h3>Sources of Graphic Noise</h3>
<ul>
<li>Multiple font families</li>
<li>Excessive bold text</li>
<li>Heavy background textures</li>
<li>Too many accent colors</li>
<li>Overlapping diagrams</li>
<li>Uncontrolled line weights</li>
<li>Overuse of gradients and shadows</li>
</ul>

Reference Study:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20minimal

<h3>Density Overload Indicators</h3>
<ul>
<li>Four equal drawings competing</li>
<li>Text blocks covering drawings</li>
<li>Diagrams without hierarchy</li>
<li>Margins reduced to fit content</li>
<li>No dominant focal point</li>
</ul>

ArchDaily Presentation Board Study:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20presentation%20board

<h3>Noise Reduction Strategy</h3>
<ul>
<li>Remove weakest drawing per page</li>
<li>Increase white space</li>
<li>Limit accent color to one</li>
<li>Standardize typography hierarchy</li>
<li>Enlarge primary drawing</li>
<li>Simplify diagram overlays</li>
</ul>

Issuu Scroll Rhythm Study:  
https://issuu.com/search?q=architecture%20portfolio

</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1600&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Graphic Noise Audit Checklist</button>
<div>
Does every page have a clear focal element?<br>
Are there more than two font families?<br>
Is color restrained?<br>
Are margins consistent?<br>
Are diagrams simplified?<br>
Does the page feel calm at 25% zoom?<br><br>
If any answer is negative, reduce and refine.
</div>
</section>

<a href="v07-ch90.html">Next — Chapter 90</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Professional Minimalism</h1>
<p>
Professional minimalism is not visual emptiness.  
It is disciplined reduction guided by hierarchy, structural clarity, and restraint.  
In architectural portfolios, minimalism communicates confidence, maturity, and control.
</p>
</section>

<section class="center-axis">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500534314209-a25ddb2bd429?q=80&w=1000&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="section">
<h3>Principles of Professional Minimalism</h3>
<ul>
<li>Clarity over decoration</li>
<li>Structure over visual experimentation</li>
<li>One dominant focal element per page</li>
<li>Restrained color usage</li>
<li>Consistent typography hierarchy</li>
<li>Controlled white space</li>
</ul>

Benchmark Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio

</section>

<section class="section">
<h3>Minimalism vs Emptiness</h3>
<ul>
<li>Minimalism retains essential information</li>
<li>Emptiness removes critical technical evidence</li>
<li>Minimal layout still shows section depth</li>
<li>Minimal color still preserves diagram clarity</li>
<li>Minimal text remains structurally precise</li>
</ul>

ArchDaily Presentation Reference:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20presentation%20minimal

</section>

<section class="section">
<h3>Common Misinterpretations</h3>
<ul>
<li>Removing dimensions entirely</li>
<li>Using extremely light grey unreadable text</li>
<li>Leaving excessive blank pages</li>
<li>Eliminating section for aesthetic simplicity</li>
<li>Prioritizing render-only presentation</li>
</ul>

Issuu Portfolio Study:  
https://issuu.com/search?q=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio

</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Minimalism Calibration Checklist</button>
<div>
Does each page have one dominant element?<br>
Are fonts limited to one or two families?<br>
Is accent color restricted?<br>
Is section visible within first five pages?<br>
Is structural clarity maintained?<br>
Does the portfolio feel calm at thumbnail view?
</div>
</section>

<a href="v07-ch91.html">Next — Chapter 91</a>
</div>

<div class="side-title">
COHESION SYSTEM
</div>

<div class="content">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Creating Cohesion Across Pages</h1>
<p>
Cohesion is the invisible thread binding a portfolio into a unified architectural narrative.  
Without cohesion, even strong individual pages appear fragmented.  
Cohesion is achieved through repetition of structure, alignment discipline, hierarchy consistency, and calibrated rhythm.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1518837695005-2083093ee35b?q=80&w=1200&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band">
<h3>Structural Cohesion</h3>
<ul>
<li>Consistent grid usage across projects</li>
<li>Uniform margin logic</li>
<li>Aligned title placement</li>
<li>Recurring page numbering system</li>
<li>Consistent annotation hierarchy</li>
</ul>

Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20consistent%20layout
</section>

<section class="band">
<h3>Visual Cohesion</h3>
<ul>
<li>Controlled color palette</li>
<li>Stable typography system</li>
<li>Uniform image treatment (grayscale or consistent tone)</li>
<li>Balanced white space rhythm</li>
<li>Consistent line weight logic</li>
</ul>

Benchmark:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=minimal%20architecture%20portfolio
</section>

<section class="band">
<h3>Narrative Cohesion</h3>
<ul>
<li>Clear sequencing from strongest to supporting work</li>
<li>Sectional evidence repeated across projects</li>
<li>Technical sheet presence</li>
<li>Progressive depth in explanation</li>
<li>Aligned specialization positioning</li>
</ul>

ArchDaily Study:  
https://www.archdaily.com/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20presentation
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Cohesion Self-Audit</button>
<div>
Do margins remain identical across all projects?<br>
Are font sizes consistent for same hierarchy level?<br>
Is project introduction placement stable?<br>
Does thumbnail scroll feel rhythmically balanced?<br>
Does one page visually disrupt the system?<br>
If yes, recalibrate.
</div>
</section>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <h1>Adobe InDesign Workflow</h1>
  <p>
  Adobe InDesign is the most structured software for architectural portfolio assembly. 
  Unlike drawing software, it manages layout systems, grids, typography hierarchy, and 
  export discipline with precision.
  </p>
  <p>
  Workflow clarity prevents layout inconsistency and repetitive manual adjustment.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="process">

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 1 — Document Setup</h3>
    <p>
    Define page size (A4 or A3). Set margins. Establish column grid. 
    Activate baseline grid. Lock master pages.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 2 — Master Page Engineering</h3>
    <p>
    Place recurring elements: title position, page numbers, header line. 
    Maintain consistency across spreads.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 3 — Placing Drawings</h3>
    <p>
    Import vector PDFs from AutoCAD/Revit. Avoid raster exports. 
    Lock image frames to prevent accidental scaling.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 4 — Typography Styles</h3>
    <p>
    Create paragraph styles: Title, Subtitle, Body, Caption. 
    Never manually format text without style definition.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 5 — Hyperlink Integration</h3>
    <p>
    Add internal navigation for index. Add clickable email and LinkedIn links. 
    Test after PDF export.
    </p>
  </div>

  <div class="step">
    <h3>Step 6 — Export Discipline</h3>
    <p>
    Use High Quality Print preset. Downsample to 150–200 DPI. 
    Embed fonts. Preserve vector data.
    </p>
  </div>

</section>

<section class="image-band">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Workflow Verification Checklist</button>
<div>
Are margins defined before layout begins?<br>
Are paragraph styles used consistently?<br>
Are vector drawings preserved?<br>
Are hyperlinks tested after export?<br>
Is file size under 15 MB?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="header-strip">
  <h1>Illustrator Workflow</h1>
  <p>
  Adobe Illustrator is primarily used for diagram construction, vector refinement, and graphic clarity enhancement. 
  It supports line precision, color discipline, and scalable export without raster degradation.
  </p>
</section>

<section class="content-grid">

<div class="left-column">

<p>
Illustrator must not replace drafting software. Instead, it refines exported CAD or Revit linework.
</p>

<p>
Primary use cases include:
</p>

<p>
● Concept diagram refinement<br>
● Environmental overlays<br>
● Lineweight adjustments<br>
● Legend creation<br>
● Graphic reduction of complex drawings
</p>

<p>
Maintain vector integrity. Avoid flattening artwork unless necessary.
</p>

<p>
Use limited color palette. Avoid excessive gradients or drop shadows.
</p>

<p>
Always align artwork to defined artboard margins matching InDesign layout.
</p>

</div>

<div class="right-column">

<div class="callout">
<h3>Vector Discipline</h3>
<p>
Maintain consistent stroke weights. Use global swatches for color control. Avoid expanding strokes unnecessarily.
</p>
</div>

<div class="callout">
<h3>Export Protocol</h3>
<p>
Export diagrams as PDF (preserve vector data) or high-resolution PNG only when raster necessary.
</p>
</div>

<div class="callout">
<h3>Common Mistakes</h3>
<p>
Overusing opacity effects.<br>
Rasterizing text.<br>
Using decorative typefaces.<br>
Inconsistent arrow styles.
</p>
</div>

</div>

</section>

<section class="image-vertical">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1522199755839-a2bacb67c546?q=80&w=1200&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Illustrator Workflow Checklist</button>
<div>
Are stroke weights consistent?<br>
Are diagrams simplified from original plan?<br>
Are colors limited to one accent?<br>
Is export vector-preserved?<br>
Does artwork align with portfolio grid?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<div class="axis">

<h1>Revit Sheet Export Discipline</h1>

<p>
Revit sheet export discipline determines whether drawings remain professional after leaving the BIM environment. 
Improper export settings lead to blurred linework, incorrect scale, broken line weights, and rasterized text.
</p>

<p>
The objective is to maintain vector clarity, consistent line hierarchy, and accurate sheet scaling.
</p>

<div class="side-note">
<h3>View Template Control</h3>
<p>
Before exporting, ensure all views use standardized view templates. 
Control cut line weight, projection line weight, and annotation scale uniformly.
</p>
</div>

<p>
Avoid exporting directly from working model views without verifying graphics overrides.
</p>

<div class="side-note">
<h3>Sheet Composition</h3>
<p>
Place views cleanly within title block margins. 
Avoid scaling views manually to fit sheet. 
Maintain consistent viewport alignment.
</p>
</div>

<div class="side-note">
<h3>PDF Export Settings</h3>
<p>
Use vector-based PDF printer. 
Disable raster processing unless necessary. 
Ensure fonts embed correctly. 
Verify line weights after export.
</p>
</div>

<div class="image-block">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1200&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Revit Export Verification Checklist</button>
<div>
Are view templates consistent?<br>
Are cut lines visually dominant?<br>
Are dimensions legible at print scale?<br>
Is PDF file vector-based?<br>
Are fonts embedded?<br>
Does file size remain under 15 MB?
</div>
</div>

<a href="v08-ch103.html">Next — Chapter 103</a>

<div class="main">

<div class="title-block">
<h1>File Optimization</h1>
<p>
File optimization ensures your portfolio opens quickly, remains sharp at zoom level, and complies with professional submission standards.
Large files reduce accessibility. Over-compressed files reduce clarity.
Balance is critical.
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Target File Size</h3>
<p>
Internship portfolio file size should ideally remain between 5 MB and 12 MB.
Files exceeding 15 MB may fail email delivery or HR portal uploads.
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Image Resolution Control</h3>
<p>
Use 150–200 DPI for digital portfolios.
Avoid placing 600 DPI renders unnecessarily.
Resize images before importing into layout software.
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Vector Preservation</h3>
<p>
Never rasterize line drawings.
Plans, sections, and text must remain vector-based.
Zoom test at 300% to verify sharpness.
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Unused Layer Cleanup</h3>
<p>
Before export:
Remove hidden layers,
Delete redundant images,
Flatten unnecessary embedded objects.
Clean files export faster.
</p>
</div>

<div class="image-wrap">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1200&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Optimization Checklist</button>
<div>
Is file under 15 MB?<br>
Are images 150–200 DPI?<br>
Are drawings vector-based?<br>
Are fonts embedded?<br>
Does PDF open instantly?<br>
Does zoom remain sharp?<br>
Have unused assets been removed?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<div class="header">
<h1>PDF Compression Strategy</h1>
<p>
PDF compression must reduce file size without degrading drawing clarity.
Over-compression causes pixelation.
Under-compression causes upload failure.
Compression must be controlled and intentional.
</p>
</div>

<div class="axis">
<div>
<h3>Why Compression Matters</h3>
<p>
Large files slow downloads and may be rejected by HR systems.
Internship submissions typically require files under 15 MB.
Balanced compression ensures accessibility without quality loss.
</p>
</div>

<div>
<h3>Common Compression Errors</h3>
<p>
Rasterizing vector drawings,
Over-reducing DPI below 100,
Using automatic online compressors without testing,
Flattening text layers improperly.
</p>
</div>
</div>

<div class="axis">
<div>
<h3>Recommended Workflow</h3>
<p>
Export using High Quality Print preset.
Downsample images to 150–200 DPI.
Preserve vector content.
Embed fonts.
Remove unnecessary metadata.
</p>
</div>

<div>
<h3>Testing After Compression</h3>
<p>
Zoom to 200%.
Check line sharpness.
Verify dimension legibility.
Confirm no color banding.
Ensure file opens quickly on mobile.
</p>
</div>
</div>

<div class="visual">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Compression Verification Checklist</button>
<div>
Is file under 15 MB?<br>
Are vector lines preserved?<br>
Are fonts embedded?<br>
Is text selectable?<br>
Are images sharp at 200% zoom?<br>
Does file preview correctly in email?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<div class="header-strip">
<h1>Naming and Version Control</h1>
<p>
File naming and version control are indicators of organizational discipline.
Before a portfolio is opened, the file name is evaluated.
Clear naming improves professionalism, traceability, and credibility.
</p>
</div>

<div class="strip">
<h3>Professional File Naming Protocol</h3>
<p>
Use structured format:
FirstName_LastName_Architecture_Internship_Portfolio_Year.pdf

Avoid:
portfolio_final.pdf  
latest_version3.pdf  
architectureportfolioNEWREALFINAL.pdf

Naming clarity prevents confusion and supports recruiter file management.
</p>
</div>

<div class="strip dark">
<h3>Version Control Discipline</h3>
<p>
Maintain organized folder system:
Portfolio_2026  
— Source Files  
— Export_PDF  
— Optimized_PDF  
— Older Versions  

Never overwrite previous files.
Track iteration clearly.
</p>
</div>

<div class="strip">
<h3>Metadata and File Integrity</h3>
<p>
Include PDF metadata:
Title  
Author  
Keywords  

Embed fonts.
Preserve vector drawings.
Ensure hyperlinks remain functional.
</p>
</div>

<div class="visual">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Version Checklist</button>
<div>
Is file name professional?<br>
Is version number clear?<br>
Are fonts embedded?<br>
Is file size under 15 MB?<br>
Are links tested?<br>
Are backups stored separately?
</div>
</div>

<aside class="index-bar">

<section class="canvas">

<h1>Choosing Page Size</h1>

<p>
Page size is not an aesthetic decision.  
It is a structural framework that determines scale legibility, drawing clarity, margin control, and physical handling efficiency.  
Incorrect page size selection weakens otherwise strong drawings.
</p>

<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1200&auto=format&fit=crop">

<div class="two-col">
<div>
<h3>A4 Strategy</h3>
<ul>
<li>Highly portable</li>
<li>Economical printing</li>
<li>Suitable for internship submissions</li>
<li>Requires disciplined density control</li>
</ul>

Common for corporate internship portfolios.

Benchmark:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20internship%20portfolio
</div>

<div>
<h3>A3 Strategy</h3>
<ul>
<li>Stronger sectional clarity</li>
<li>Better plan readability</li>
<li>More breathing space</li>
<li>Greater physical presence</li>
</ul>

Recommended when technical drawings dominate.

Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20A3
</div>
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Page Size Decision Checklist</button>
<div>
Does your section lose clarity when reduced to A4?<br>
Are dimensions readable at actual print scale?<br>
Is portfolio meant for digital-only submission?<br>
Does your grid system scale proportionally?<br>
Have you printed one sample test page?
</div>
</div>

  margin-left:220px;
  padding:80px 0;

<div class="main">

<section class="hero-band">
<h1>Portrait vs Landscape Strategy</h1>

<p>
Orientation is not stylistic preference.  
It determines drawing dominance, sectional legibility, and horizontal or vertical narrative rhythm.  
The choice must align with drawing type and presentation objective.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="offset-section">
<div>
<h3>Portrait Orientation</h3>
<ul>
<li>Stronger vertical hierarchy</li>
<li>Suitable for section-dominant portfolios</li>
<li>More natural for reading-heavy layouts</li>
<li>Better for academic documentation</li>
</ul>

Behance Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20portrait
</div>

<div>
Portrait pages emphasize vertical stacking of elements.  
Sections align naturally.  
Text hierarchy flows top to bottom.  
However, large horizontal plans may reduce in clarity.
</div>
</section>

<section class="offset-section">
<div>
Landscape pages favor horizontal expansion.  
Plans breathe.  
Wide sections gain clarity.  
Mapping and urban diagrams benefit significantly.
</div>

<div>
<h3>Landscape Orientation</h3>
<ul>
<li>Stronger plan visibility</li>
<li>Balanced side-by-side comparisons</li>
<li>Better for urban and mapping projects</li>
<li>Common in professional portfolios</li>
</ul>

Behance Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20landscape
</div>
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Orientation Decision Checklist</button>
<div>
Does your primary drawing type require horizontal expansion?<br>
Is your section more vertical than horizontal?<br>
Will plans lose clarity when scaled down?<br>
Does your chosen grid system adapt to orientation?<br>
Have you printed one test sheet?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>A4 vs A3 Logic</h1>

<p>
Page size is not cosmetic.  
It directly affects drawing scale, detail clarity, margin proportion, and reviewer handling.  
Choosing between A4 and A3 must be based on structural content rather than preference.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-block">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="split-container">

<div class="panel left">
<h3>A4 Logic</h3>

<ul>
<li>Most common internship format</li>
<li>Portable and economical</li>
<li>Compatible with HR filing systems</li>
<li>Requires tighter density control</li>
</ul>

Limitations:
<ul>
<li>Reduced plan legibility</li>
<li>Smaller section visibility</li>
<li>Detail drawings require careful scaling</li>
</ul>

Behance Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20A4
</div>

<div class="panel right">
<h3>A3 Logic</h3>

<ul>
<li>Greater technical clarity</li>
<li>Stronger sectional representation</li>
<li>Improved drawing hierarchy</li>
<li>Professional visual impact</li>
</ul>

Limitations:
<ul>
<li>Less portable</li>
<li>Higher printing cost</li>
<li>Some firms prefer A4 submissions</li>
</ul>

Behance Reference:  
https://www.behance.net/search/projects?search=architecture%20portfolio%20A3
</div>

</section>

<section class="axis-strip">
Scale clarity should determine page size — not visual preference.
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Page Size Decision Checklist</button>
<div>
Are your sections readable at A4 scale?<br>
Do your details require 1:20 clarity?<br>
Will HR require compact documentation?<br>
Have you printed one test page at actual size?<br>
Does your grid system adapt proportionally?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Template Archetypes</h1>

<p>
A template is not decoration.  
It is a structural framework that defines margin logic, title placement, grid hierarchy, and 
repetition discipline across pages.  
Template selection determines whether a portfolio feels engineered or improvised.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1518779578993-ec3579fee39f?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<div class="floating-bar">
<span>Minimal</span>
<span>Editorial</span>
<span>Technical</span>
<span>Bold Visual</span>
</div>

<section class="matrix">

<div class="block">
<h3>Minimal Template</h3>
<ul>
<li>Strict margins</li>
<li>One-column structure</li>
<li>Neutral typography</li>
<li>White background</li>
</ul>
Best suited for corporate-oriented portfolios.
</div>

<div class="block">
<h3>Editorial Template</h3>
<ul>
<li>Two-column system</li>
<li>Text + drawing integration</li>
<li>Controlled asymmetry</li>
<li>Balanced white space</li>
</ul>
Suitable for boutique and concept-driven studios.
</div>

<div class="block">
<h3>Technical Template</h3>
<ul>
<li>Strong grid visibility</li>
<li>Clear title block structure</li>
<li>Dimension priority</li>
<li>Reduced render dominance</li>
</ul>
Aligned with BIM-heavy firms.
</div>

<div class="block">
<h3>Bold Visual Template</h3>
<ul>
<li>Large dominant image</li>
<li>Controlled typography overlay</li>
<li>High contrast composition</li>
<li>Minimal text blocks</li>
</ul>
Effective only when drawings remain clear.
</div>

</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Template Selection Checklist</button>
<div>
Does your template align with target firm type?<br>
Is grid system locked across pages?<br>
Are margins consistent?<br>
Is hierarchy visible at 25% zoom?<br>
Does template overpower content?
</div>
</div>

  <a href="../index-book.html">Master Index</a>

  <a href="../contact.html">Contact</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="header">
<h1>Minimal Template System</h1>
<p>
A minimal template is a controlled structural system that eliminates unnecessary graphic noise and 
prioritizes clarity. It operates through strict margin control, limited typography, and a reduced visual 
palette. Minimalism in portfolio design is not aesthetic emptiness; it is deliberate reduction to enhance 
architectural communication.
</p>
</section>

<section class="abstract-image">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1526318472351-bc6c7d8b3f99?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="central-axis">

<div class="main-text">

<p>
Core Principles of a Minimal Template:
</p>

<p>
● Strict grid alignment<br>
● Neutral color palette<br>
● Generous white space<br>
● One primary font family<br>
● Controlled hierarchy
</p>

<p>
Minimal templates are particularly effective for technical portfolios and corporate-oriented 
submissions where structural clarity must remain dominant. Excessive decorative elements dilute 
perceived professionalism.
</p>

<p>
Page Composition Strategy:
</p>

<p>
Each page should contain one dominant drawing element supported by limited secondary content. 
Overcrowding contradicts minimal logic. The template must reinforce discipline across the entire 
portfolio.
</p>

</div>

<div class="side-notes">
<strong>Best Used For:</strong><br>
Corporate firms<br>
Technical-heavy portfolios<br>
BIM-focused submissions<br><br>

<strong>Avoid If:</strong><br>
Portfolio relies on expressive graphic identity<br>
Heavy diagram layering required<br>
Visual experimentation is central
</div>

</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Minimal Template Evaluation Checklist</button>
<div>
Are margins consistent across all pages?<br>
Is only one font family used?<br>
Does white space dominate rather than content?<br>
Is hierarchy visible at thumbnail scale?<br>
Does layout remain calm when scrolling?
</div>
</div>

<div class="chapter-nav">
<a href="v08-ch95.html">Previous — Chapter 95</a>
<a href="v08-ch97.html">Next — Chapter 97</a>
</div>

  display:grid;
  grid-template-columns:1fr 1fr;
  gap:80px;

  border-bottom:1px solid #f0f0f0;

  margin-bottom:22px;

  <a href="../contact.html">Contact</a>

<div class="main">

<section class="header-strip">
<h1>Editorial Template System</h1>
<p>
An editorial template system borrows logic from magazine layout structures. It emphasizes narrative 
flow, controlled asymmetry, and carefully composed image-text relationships. Unlike minimal systems 
that prioritize restraint alone, editorial systems prioritize storytelling through spatial sequencing.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-panel">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500530855697-b586d89ba3ee?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="band">
<div>
<p>
Core Characteristics:
</p>
<p>
● Structured but flexible grid<br>
● Balanced asymmetry<br>
● Controlled typography contrast<br>
● Integrated captions<br>
● Visual pacing between spreads
</p>
</div>
<div>
<p>
Editorial templates are suitable for portfolios where narrative clarity and visual engagement are 
equally important. Boutique studios and concept-driven firms often respond positively to editorial 
composition when it remains disciplined.
</p>
</div>
</section>

<section class="band">
<div>
<p>
Page Flow Strategy:
</p>
<p>
Alternate density levels. Pair diagram-heavy pages with calmer text pages. Avoid placing two visually 
dense spreads consecutively. Maintain rhythm across project transitions.
</p>
</div>
<div>
<p>
Hierarchy remains critical. Despite expressive layout freedom, grid discipline must remain intact. 
Typography should never overpower drawings. Images must retain structural clarity.
</p>
</div>
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Editorial Template Evaluation Checklist</button>
<div>
Is the narrative flow coherent across pages?<br>
Are asymmetrical compositions anchored to grid?<br>
Is typography controlled and consistent?<br>
Do image-caption relationships remain clear?<br>
Does visual rhythm feel intentional rather than random?
</div>
</div>

<a href="v08-ch98.html">Next — Chapter 98</a>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Technical Template System</h1>
<p>
A technical template system prioritizes drawing clarity, structural hierarchy, and disciplined 
annotation. It minimizes decorative layout gestures and focuses on measurable representation. This 
system is most effective when applying to execution-driven or corporate architectural firms.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1503387762-592deb58ef4e?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="module">
<div>
<p>
Core Characteristics:
</p>
<p>
● Structured grid with fixed margins<br>
● Clear line weight hierarchy<br>
● Dimension discipline<br>
● Minimal color usage<br>
● Section dominance over renders
</p>
<p>
Technical templates are drawing-forward systems. Plans and sections receive primary spatial emphasis. 
Renders support rather than dominate.
</p>
</div>
<div class="side-note">
Technical templates reduce expressive variation. 
Consistency and legibility override aesthetic experimentation.
</div>
</section>

<section class="module">
<div>
<p>
Page Composition Strategy:
</p>
<p>
Each page should contain one dominant technical drawing. Supporting diagrams and annotations remain 
subordinate. Avoid clustering multiple small drawings with equal weight.
</p>
<p>
Include scale indication, north arrow, and dimension strings clearly. Maintain consistent annotation 
style across projects.
</p>
</div>
<div class="side-note">
Sections are often the strongest proof of technical maturity. 
Place them early in the project sequence.
</div>
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Technical Template Evaluation Checklist</button>
<div>
Are plans dimensioned appropriately?<br>
Is section structurally believable?<br>
Is line weight hierarchy visible?<br>
Is typography minimal and disciplined?<br>
Is color usage restrained?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
  <div class="hero-left">
    <h1>Bold Visual Template System</h1>
    <p>
    A bold visual template system prioritizes strong visual impact while maintaining structural discipline. 
    It allows expressive composition, larger imagery, and dynamic layout shifts, yet remains grounded in 
    grid logic and hierarchy control.
    </p>
  </div>
  <div class="hero-right">
    <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1487958449943-2429e8be8625?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
  </div>
</section>

<section class="offset-section">
  <div class="offset-box">
    <p>
    Core Characteristics:
    </p>
    <p>
    ● Large-scale visuals<br>
    ● Controlled asymmetry<br>
    ● Strong typographic contrast<br>
    ● Minimal but high-impact color accents<br>
    ● Clear focal dominance per page
    </p>
    <p>
    Bold systems are most suitable for design-led studios, competition-focused practices, or portfolios 
    emphasizing conceptual exploration.
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<section class="overlay-block">
  <img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1501594907352-04cda38ebc29?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
  <div class="overlay-text">
    <p>
    Even in expressive layouts, hierarchy must remain visible. One dominant element per page ensures 
    clarity. Without control, bold design quickly becomes graphic noise.
    </p>
  </div>
</section>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Bold Template Evaluation Checklist</button>
<div>
Is there one dominant focal element per page?<br>
Is asymmetry controlled by grid logic?<br>
Are color accents limited and intentional?<br>
Does typography remain disciplined?<br>
Is technical credibility still visible?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<div class="header">
<div>
<h1>Resume Architecture</h1>
<p>
The resume is not a separate document.
It is a structural layer of professional identity.
Within internship applications, the resume reinforces the portfolio narrative.
It must communicate clarity, technical readiness, and alignment.
</p>
</div>
<div>
<p>
The resume must:
● Reinforce technical credibility  
● Align with portfolio archetype  
● Confirm software proficiency  
● Communicate internship readiness  
● Provide quick-reference summary  

It must not:
● Repeat portfolio content  
● Include decorative elements  
● Become text-heavy  
● Disrupt layout consistency  
</p>
</div>
</div>

<div class="visual">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1500534314209-a25ddb2bd429?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Placement Strategy</h3>
<p>
Option A – After Cover  
Cover  
Resume  
Index  
Projects  

Best for corporate-oriented portfolios.

Option B – After Index  
Cover  
Index  
Resume  
Projects  

Suitable for boutique or design-led portfolios.
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Resume Content Structure</h3>
<p>
Include:
● Name  
● Contact information  
● Education  
● Internship experience  
● Technical skills  
● Software proficiency  
● Workshops / Competitions (optional)  

Avoid:
● Long objective paragraphs  
● Decorative icons  
● Irrelevant extracurricular content  
</p>
</div>

<div class="section">
<h3>Software Section Alignment</h3>
<p>
Software must reflect portfolio evidence.

If working drawings included → AutoCAD / Revit  
If environmental diagrams included → analysis tools  
If parametric models included → Rhino / Grasshopper  
If interior detailing included → SketchUp  

Resume credibility depends on visible alignment.
</p>
</div>

<div class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Final Resume Integration Checklist</button>
<div>
Is resume one page only?<br>
Does margin system match portfolio?<br>
Are fonts consistent?<br>
Are skills defensible?<br>
Is resume aligned with target firm expectations?
</div>
</div>

<div class="main">

<section class="hero">
<h1>Resume Graphic Discipline</h1>
<p>
A resume must reflect the same graphic discipline as the portfolio.
It is not a separate visual language.
Graphic inconsistency between resume and portfolio reduces credibility.
</p>
</section>

<section class="image-strip">
<img src="https://images.unsplash.com/photo-1492724441997-5dc865305da7?q=80&w=1400&auto=format&fit=crop">
</section>

<section class="strip">
<div>
<h3>Typography Control</h3>
<p>
Maintain identical font family used in portfolio.
Define three levels only:
Primary – Name  
Secondary – Section headings  
Tertiary – Body text  

Avoid:
● Multiple fonts  
● Decorative styles  
● Overly bold headings  
</p>
</div>
<div>
<h3>Margin and Grid Consistency</h3>
<p>
Resume must follow the same grid logic as portfolio.

Check:
● Margin alignment  
● Title alignment  
● Equal spacing  
● Consistent section breaks  

Resume layout should feel structurally engineered.
</p>
</div>
</section>

<section class="strip">
<div>
<h3>Visual Weight Calibration</h3>
<p>
Name must be dominant.
Section headings moderate.
Body text neutral.

Avoid excessive graphic icons.
Avoid colored bars unless subtle.
White space must remain controlled.
</p>
</div>
<div>
<h3>Information Density Control</h3>
<p>
Keep resume one page.
Avoid dense paragraphs.
Use bullet structure.

Recommended structure:
Education  
Experience  
Skills  
Software  
Competitions / Workshops  
</p>
</div>
</section>

<section class="accordion">
<button onclick="toggleAcc(this)">Graphic Integrity Checklist</button>
<div>
Is font identical to portfolio?<br>
Are margins consistent?<br>
Is hierarchy clear?<br>
Is white space balanced?<br>
Does resume feel architecturally structured?
</div>
</section>

<a href="v09-ch108.html">Next — Chapter 108</a>
</section>
